<p><i><b>Gladiator</b></i> is a 2000 British-American <a href="/wiki/Epic_film" title="Epic film">epic</a> <a href="/wiki/Historical_period_drama" title="Historical period drama">historical drama film</a> directed by <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a>, and starring <a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe" title="Russell Crowe">Russell Crowe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joaquin_Phoenix" title="Joaquin Phoenix">Joaquin Phoenix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connie_Nielsen" title="Connie Nielsen">Connie Nielsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralf_M%C3%B6ller" title="Ralf Möller">Ralf Möller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Reed" title="Oliver Reed">Oliver Reed</a> (in his final film role before his death), <a href="/wiki/Djimon_Hounsou" title="Djimon Hounsou">Djimon Hounsou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derek_Jacobi" title="Derek Jacobi">Derek Jacobi</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Shrapnel" title="John Shrapnel">John Shrapnel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Harris" title="Richard Harris">Richard Harris</a>. Crowe portrays the fictional character, loyal <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> general Maximus Decimus Meridius, who is betrayed when <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, the ambitious son of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>, murders his father and seizes the throne. Reduced to slavery, Maximus rises through the ranks of the gladiatorial arena to avenge the murders of his family and his emperor.</p>
<p>The film was released in the United States on May 5, 2000, and was a box office success, receiving generally positive reviews and being credited with rekindling interest in the historical epic. The film won multiple awards, including <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Best Picture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Actor">Best Actor</a> for Crowe, and three other <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Oscars</a> at the <a href="/wiki/73rd_Academy_Awards" title="73rd Academy Awards">73rd Academy Awards</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a> 180, <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispano-Roman</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General" title="General">General</a> Maximus Decimus Meridius leads the <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> to a decisive victory against the <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germanic</a> tribes near <a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a>, ending a <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">long war on the Roman frontier</a> and winning the favor of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>. Maximus, weary of battle, desires to retire to his Spanish farm estate, but the Emperor tells him that his own son and heir to the throne, <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, is unfit to rule and so appoints Maximus as regent to help save <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> from corruption. Marcus Aurelius tells Commodus that he will not be emperor; Commodus murders him in a fit of rage and becomes the Emperor of Rome.</p>
<p>Commodus asks Maximus for his loyalty, but the general suspects foul play and refuses. Soon after, Maximus is arrested and is scheduled to be executed at dawn. Maximus manages to escape and makes the long journey to his farm on horseback, but arrives to find it burnt and his family dead, under orders of Commodus. He buries them and collapses in despair. He is then captured by slavers who take him to <a href="/wiki/Zuccabar" title="Zuccabar">Zucchabar</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> province. He is sold to a man named Proximo, who trains him as a <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiator</a>. There he befriends two of Proximo's gladiators, a <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidian</a> named Juba and a <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> named Hagen.</p>
<p>Reluctant at first, Maximus is forced to fight in local tournaments. He wins every match because of his superior military skills and traumatized indifference to death. His newfound fame and recognition is brought to Proximo's attention. Proximo reveals to Maximus that he himself was once a gladiator, and had fought well enough to have gained his freedom. He encourages Maximus to go to Rome and fight in the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>, where the Emperor has organized 150 days of games to commemorate his late father. Proximo advises him to "win the crowd" to earn their respect and eventually his freedom. He could then use this leverage to possibly overthrow or kill the Emperor as part of his plan for revenge.</p>
<p>Maximus' first taste of gladiatorial combat in the Colosseum is depicted as a re-enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">Battle of Zama</a>. With his team portrayed as <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginian</a> infantry, they defy all expectations and are able (solely due to Maximus' commands) to defeat "<a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">Scipio</a>'s legionaries" who were supposed to obliterate them. A surprised and delighted Commodus comes into the arena to personally congratulate them. Maximus braces himself to kill the Emperor, but at the last moment decides against it because of the presence of Commodus' young nephew Lucius Verus. He then reveals himself to the startled Commodus and vows to have his vengeance. As the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a> prepares to kill him, the crowd chants "Live!" repeatedly, earning their favor and showing their support. Commodus relents and leaves angrily.</p>
<p>Next, Maximus is pitted against the formidable Tigris of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, an undefeated gladiator. After a fierce and lengthy duel which Commodus attempted to fix, Maximus is able to gain the upper hand, but spares his opponent's life despite the crowd's urging that he kill him. Angered by this action, Commodus berates him, even taunting him with harrowing details of his family's death. Undaunted, Maximus turns around and walks away. This act of defiance, along with his victory over Tigris, makes him more popular than the Emperor himself.</p>
<p>As Maximus is being escorted back to the gladiators' quarters, his former orderly Cicero approaches him and says that he still has the loyalty of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">legions</a>, encamped near Rome. Commodus' sister <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Lucilla</a> and the chief senator Gracchus secure a meeting with Maximus, and he obtains their promise to help him escape Rome, rejoin his soldiers, topple Commodus by force, and hand power over back to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a>. Suspicious, Commodus learns of this plot from Lucilla by threatening young Lucius, then dispatches his men to arrest and/or kill the conspirators. Gracchus is quickly apprehended, while a contingent of Praetorians is sent to Maximus' quarters. Proximo lets Maximus escape and sacrifices himself and his men (including Hagen) to gain him more time. Maximus reaches the rendezvous point but falls into a trap; Cicero is killed and Maximus is captured.</p>
<p>Commodus, desperate and jealous of Maximus' growing popularity, challenges him to a duel in the Colosseum. Before the fight, and unknown to the crowd, he stabs him in the side to put him at a disadvantage. During the fight, Maximus manages to evade Commodus' blows and disarm him. Commodus asks the Praetorians to give him a sword, but his request is denied. He produces a hidden <a href="/wiki/Stiletto" title="Stiletto">stiletto</a>, but Maximus instinctively turns the blade back into his throat, killing him.</p>
<p>However, Maximus succumbs to the stab wound, asking with his last words that reforms be made, his gladiator allies freed, and that Senator Gracchus be reinstated. He is then carried away for an honorable funeral as a "soldier of Rome", while Commodus' corpse is left unceremoniously on the ground. Later, Juba revisits the Colosseum at night, now free to go back to his own family, and he buries Maximus' figurines of his wife and son at the spot where he died. He promises that he will see Maximus again, "but not yet".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cast">Cast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Cast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe" title="Russell Crowe">Russell Crowe</a> as Maximus Decimus Meridius: a <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispano-Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Legatus" title="Legatus">legatus</a> forced into becoming a slave who seeks revenge against Commodus. He has earned the favor of Marcus Aurelius, and the love and admiration of Lucilla prior to the events of the film. His home is near <a href="/wiki/Trujillo,_C%C3%A1ceres" title="Trujillo, Cáceres">Trujillo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> in today's <a href="/wiki/Province_of_C%C3%A1ceres" title="Province of Cáceres">Province of Cáceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. After the murder of his family he vows vengeance. Maximus is a fictional character partly inspired by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Nonius_Macrinus" title="Marcus Nonius Macrinus">Marcus Nonius Macrinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(wrestler)" title="Narcissus (wrestler)">Narcissus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Quinctius_Cincinnatus" title="Lucius Quinctius Cincinnatus">Cincinnatus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maximus_of_Hispania" title="Maximus of Hispania">Maximus of Hispania</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mel_Gibson" title="Mel Gibson">Mel Gibson</a> was first offered the role,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> but declined as he felt he was too old to play the character. <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Banderas" title="Antonio Banderas">Antonio Banderas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Jackman" title="Hugh Jackman">Hugh Jackman</a> were also considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Joaquin_Phoenix" title="Joaquin Phoenix">Joaquin Phoenix</a> as <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>: The corrupted, twisted and immoral son of Marcus Aurelius, he murders his father when he learns that Maximus will hold the emperor's powers in trust until a new republic can be formed.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Connie_Nielsen" title="Connie Nielsen">Connie Nielsen</a> as Lucilla: Maximus's former lover and the older child of Marcus Aurelius. Lucilla has been recently widowed. She resists her brother's <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incestuous</a> advances for she hates him, while also having to be careful to protect her son, Lucius, from her brother's corruption and wrath.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Reed" title="Oliver Reed">Oliver Reed</a> as Antonius Proximo: An old, gruff gladiator trainer who buys Maximus in North Africa. A former gladiator himself, he was freed by Marcus Aurelius, and gives Maximus his own armor and eventually a chance at freedom, and becomes somewhat of a mentor to Maximus. This was Reed's final film appearance, before he died during the filming. In the original film script, Proximo was supposed to live.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Derek_Jacobi" title="Derek Jacobi">Derek Jacobi</a> as Senator Gracchus: A member of the Roman Senate who opposes Commodus's rule and an ally of Lucilla and Maximus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Djimon_Hounsou" title="Djimon Hounsou">Djimon Hounsou</a> as Juba: A <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidian</a> tribesman who was taken from his home and family by slave traders. He becomes Maximus's closest ally and friend.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Schofield_(actor)" title="David Schofield (actor)">David Schofield</a> as Senator Falco: A <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">Patrician</a>, a senator opposed to Gracchus. He helps Commodus to consolidate his power.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Shrapnel" title="John Shrapnel">John Shrapnel</a> as Senator Gaius: Another Roman senator allied with Gracchus, Lucilla, and Maximus against Commodus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tomas_Arana" title="Tomas Arana">Tomas Arana</a> as General Quintus: another Roman <a href="/wiki/Legatus" title="Legatus">legatus</a>, who served under and was a friend to Maximus. Made commander of the Praetorian Guard by Commodus, after betraying Maximus. In the extended version, Quintus sees the mad side of the Emperor when he is forced to execute two innocent men. Quintus later redeems himself by refusing to allow Commodus a sword during his duel with Maximus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ralf_M%C3%B6ller" title="Ralf Möller">Ralf Möller</a> as Hagen: A <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> warrior and Proximo's chief gladiator who later befriends Maximus and Juba during their battles in Rome.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spencer_Treat_Clark" title="Spencer Treat Clark">Spencer Treat Clark</a> as Lucius Verus: The young son of Lucilla. He is named after his father <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>. He is also the grandson of Marcus Aurelius.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Hemmings" title="David Hemmings">David Hemmings</a> as Cassius: the master of ceremonies for the gladiatorial games in the Colosseum.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tommy_Flanagan_(actor)" title="Tommy Flanagan (actor)">Tommy Flanagan</a> as Cicero: Maximus's loyal servant who provides liaison between the enslaved Maximus, his former legion based at <a href="/wiki/Ostia_Antica" title="Ostia Antica">Ostia</a>, and Lucilla. He is used as bait for the escaping Maximus and eventually killed by the Praetorian Guard.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sven-Ole_Thorsen" title="Sven-Ole Thorsen">Sven-Ole Thorsen</a> as Tigris of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>: An undefeated champion gladiator who is called out of retirement by Commodus to kill Maximus but is defeated by Maximus. Commodus then ordered Maximus to kill Tigris, but he spared him, which caused Commodus to hate Maximus even more.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Harris" title="Richard Harris">Richard Harris</a> as <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>: The old and wise emperor of Rome who appoints Maximus, whom he loves as a son, with the ultimate aim of returning Rome to a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">republican form of government</a>. He is murdered by his son Commodus before his wish can be fulfilled.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Omid_Djalili" title="Omid Djalili">Omid Djalili</a> as a slave trader.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Giannina_Facio" title="Giannina Facio">Giannina Facio</a> as Maximus's wife.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Cantarini" title="Giorgio Cantarini">Giorgio Cantarini</a> as Maximus's son who is the same age as Lucilla's son Lucius.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Screenplay">Screenplay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Screenplay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Gladiator</i> was based on an original pitch by <a href="/wiki/David_Franzoni" title="David Franzoni">David Franzoni</a>, who wrote the first draft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stax_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stax-8">[8]</a></sup> Franzoni was given a three-picture deal with <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks" title="DreamWorks">DreamWorks</a> as writer and co-producer on the strength of his previous work, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Amistad_(film)" title="Amistad (film)">Amistad</a></i>, which helped establish the reputation of DreamWorks. Not a classical scholar, Franzoni was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_P._Mannix" title="Daniel P. Mannix">Daniel P. Mannix</a>'s 1958 novel <i>Those About to Die,</i> and he chose to base his story on Commodus after reading the <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Augustan History</a></i>. In Franzoni's first draft, dated April 4, 1998, he named his protagonist <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(wrestler)" title="Narcissus (wrestler)">Narcissus</a>, a wrestler who, according to the ancient sources <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, strangled Emperor Commodus to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FilmHistoryWinkler_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FilmHistoryWinkler-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg"><img alt='Several dead men and various scattered weapons are located in a large arena. Near the center of the image is a man wearing armor standing in the middle of an arena looking up at a large crowd. The man has his right foot on the throat of an injured man who is reaching towards the crowd. Members of the crowd are indicating a "thumbs down" gesture. The arena is adorned with marble, columns, flags, and statues.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1630" data-file-width="2425" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg/220px-Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg/330px-Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg/440px-Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean-Leon_Gerome_Pollice_Verso.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Pollice_Verso_(G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me)" title="Pollice Verso (Gérôme)">Pollice Verso (Thumbs Down)</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me" title="Jean-Léon Gérôme">Jean-Léon Gérôme</a>, the 19th-century painting that inspired Ridley Scott to tackle the project.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a> was approached by producers <a href="/wiki/Walter_Parkes" title="Walter Parkes">Walter F. Parkes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Wick" title="Douglas Wick">Douglas Wick</a>. They showed him a copy of <a href="/wiki/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me" title="Jean-Léon Gérôme">Jean-Léon Gérôme</a>'s 1872 painting entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Pollice_Verso_(G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me)" title="Pollice Verso (Gérôme)">Pollice Verso (Thumbs Down)</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_22_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_22-10">[10]</a></sup> Scott was enticed by filming the world of Ancient Rome. However, Scott felt Franzoni's dialogue was too "on the nose" (lacking subtlety) and hired <a href="/wiki/John_Logan_(writer)" title="John Logan (writer)">John Logan</a> to rewrite the script to his liking. Logan rewrote much of the first act, and made the decision to kill off Maximus's family to increase the character's motivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribes_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-scribes-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe" title="Russell Crowe">Russell Crowe</a> describes being eager for the role as pitched by Walter F. Parkes, in his interview for <i><a href="/wiki/Inside_the_Actors_Studio" title="Inside the Actors Studio">Inside the Actors Studio</a></i>: "They said, 'It's a 100-million-dollar film. You're being directed by Ridley Scott. You play a Roman General.' I've always been a big fan of Ridley's."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaspinet.com_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaspinet.com-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>With two weeks to go before filming, the actors complained of problems with the script. <a href="/wiki/William_Nicholson_(writer)" title="William Nicholson (writer)">William Nicholson</a> was brought to <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Studios" title="Shepperton Studios">Shepperton Studios</a> to make Maximus a more sensitive character, reworking his friendship with Juba and developed the afterlife thread in the film, saying "he did not want to see a film about a man who wanted to kill somebody."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribes_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-scribes-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The screenplay faced many rewrites and revisions. Crowe allegedly questioned every aspect of the evolving script and strode off the set when he did not get answers. According to a DreamWorks executive, "(Russell Crowe) tried to rewrite the entire script on the spot. You know the big line in the trailer, 'In this life or the next, I will have my vengeance'? At first he absolutely refused to say it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corliss2000_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corliss2000-13">[13]</a></sup> Nicholson, the third and final screenwriter, says Crowe told him, "Your lines are garbage but I'm the greatest actor in the world, and I can make even garbage sound good." Nicholson goes on to say that "probably my lines were garbage, so he was just talking straight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref_2006_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2006-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Russell Crowe described the script situation: "I read the script and it was substantially underdone. Even the character didn't exist on the pages. And that set about a long process, that's probably the first time that I've been in a situation where the script wasn't a complete done deal. We actually started shooting with about 32 pages and went through them in the first couple of weeks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaspinet.com_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-kaspinet.com-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the writing/filming process, Crowe added, "Possibly, a lot of the stuff that I have to deal with now in terms of my quote unquote volatility has to do with that experience. Here was a situation where we got to Morocco with a crew of 200 and a cast of a 100 or whatever, and I didn't have anything to learn. I actually didn't know what the scenes were gonna be. We had, I think, one American writer working on it, one English writer working on it, and of course a group of producers who were also adding their ideas, and then Ridley himself; and then, on the occasion where Ridley would say, 'Look, this is the structure for it -- what are you gonna say in that?' So then I'd be doing my own stuff, as well. And this is how things like, 'Strength and honor,' came up. This is how things like, 'At my signal, unleash hell,' came up. The name Maximus Decimus Meridius, it just flowed well."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaspinet.com_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-kaspinet.com-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-production">Pre-production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pre-production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In preparation for filming, Scott spent several months developing storyboards to develop the framework of the plot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_34_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_34-15">[15]</a></sup> Over six weeks, production members scouted various locations within the extent of the Roman Empire before its collapse, including Italy, France, North Africa, and England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_61_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_61-16">[16]</a></sup> All of the film's props, sets, and costumes were manufactured by crew members due to high costs and unavailability of the items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_66_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_66-17">[17]</a></sup> One hundred suits of steel armour and 550 suits in polyurethane were made by Rod Vass and his company Armordillo. The unique sprayed-polyurethane system was developed by Armordillo and pioneered for this production. Over a three-month period, 27,500 component pieces of armor were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Filming">Filming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Filming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The film was shot in three main locations between January and May 1999. The opening battle scenes in the forests of Germania were shot in three weeks in the <a href="/wiki/Bourne_Wood" title="Bourne Wood">Bourne Woods</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Farnham" title="Farnham">Farnham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a> in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_62_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_62-19">[19]</a></sup> When Scott learned that the <a href="/wiki/Forestry_Commission" title="Forestry Commission">Forestry Commission</a> planned to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">remove</a> a section of the forest, he convinced them to allow the battle scene to be shot there and burn it down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_68_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_68-20">[20]</a></sup> Scott and cinematographer John Mathieson used multiple cameras filming at various frame rates and a <a href="/wiki/Rotary_disc_shutter" title="Rotary disc shutter">45-degree shutter</a>, creating <a href="/wiki/Stop_motion" title="Stop motion">stop motion</a> effects in the action sequences, similar to techniques used for the battle sequences of <i><a href="/wiki/Saving_Private_Ryan" title="Saving Private Ryan">Saving Private Ryan</a></i> (1998).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bankston2000_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bankston2000-21">[21]</a></sup> Subsequently, the scenes of slavery, desert travel, and gladiatorial training school were shot in <a href="/wiki/Ouarzazate" title="Ouarzazate">Ouarzazate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Morocco#Movies_in_Morocco" title="Culture of Morocco">Morocco</a> just south of the Atlas Mountains over a further three weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_63_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_63-22">[22]</a></sup> To construct the arena where Maximus has his first fights, the crew used basic materials and local building techniques to manufacture the 30,000-seat mud brick arena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_73_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_73-23">[23]</a></sup> Finally, the scenes of Ancient Rome were shot over a period of nineteen weeks in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Ricasoli" title="Fort Ricasoli">Fort Ricasoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kodak_inCamera_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kodak_inCamera-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mfc_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-mfc-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Malta, a replica of about one-third of Rome's Colosseum was built, to a height of 52 feet (15.8 meters), mostly from <a href="/wiki/Plaster" title="Plaster">plaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plywood" title="Plywood">plywood</a> (the other two-thirds and remaining height were added digitally).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_89_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_89-26">[26]</a></sup> The replica took several months to build and cost an estimated $1 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_p130_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_p130-27">[27]</a></sup> The reverse side of the complex supplied a rich assortment of Ancient Roman street furniture, <a href="/wiki/Colonnade" title="Colonnade">colonnades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gate" title="Gate">gates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statue" title="Statue">statuary</a>, and marketplaces for other filming requirements. The complex was serviced by tented "costume villages" that had changing rooms, storage, armorers, and other facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kodak_inCamera_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kodak_inCamera-24">[24]</a></sup> The rest of the Colosseum was created in <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated imagery</a> using set-design blueprints and textures referenced from live action, and rendered in three layers to provide lighting flexibility for compositing in <a href="/wiki/Autodesk_Media_and_Entertainment" title="Autodesk Media and Entertainment">Flame</a> and <a href="/wiki/Autodesk_Media_and_Entertainment" title="Autodesk Media and Entertainment">Inferno</a> software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMill_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheMill-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-production">Post-production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cityrome.jpg"><img alt="Men in white robes with the Colosseum in the background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/74/Cityrome.jpg/220px-Cityrome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/74/Cityrome.jpg/330px-Cityrome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/74/Cityrome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cityrome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Several scenes included extensive use of computer-generated imagery shots for views of Rome.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>British post-production company <a href="/wiki/The_Mill_(post-production)" title="The Mill (post-production)">The Mill</a> was responsible for much of the computer-generated imagery effects that were added after filming. The company was responsible for such tricks as compositing real tigers filmed on <a href="/wiki/Chroma_key" title="Chroma key">bluescreen</a> into the fight sequences, and adding smoke trails and extending the flight paths of the opening scene's salvo of flaming arrows to get around regulations on how far they could be shot during filming. They also used 2,000 live actors to create a computer-generated crowd of 35,000 virtual actors that had to look believable and react to fight scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The Mill accomplished this by shooting live actors at different angles giving various performances, and then mapping them onto cards, with <a href="/wiki/Motion_capture" title="Motion capture">motion-capture tools</a> used to track their movements for three-dimensional compositing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMill_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-TheMill-28">[28]</a></sup> The Mill created over 90 visual effects shots, comprising approximately nine minutes of the film's running time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_122_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_122-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>An unexpected post-production job was caused by the death of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Reed" title="Oliver Reed">Oliver Reed</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a> during the filming in Malta, before all his scenes had been shot. The Mill created a digital <a href="/wiki/Body_double" title="Body double">body double</a> for the remaining scenes involving his character Proximo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMill_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-TheMill-28">[28]</a></sup> by photographing a live action body-double in the shadows and by mapping a three-dimensional computer-generated imagery mask of Reed's face to the remaining scenes during production at an estimated cost of $3.2 million for two minutes of additional footage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_123_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_123-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ORtwo_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ORtwo-32">[32]</a></sup> Visual effects supervisor <a href="/wiki/John_Nelson_(visual_effects_artist)" title="John Nelson (visual effects artist)">John Nelson</a> reflected on the decision to include the additional footage: "What we did was small compared to our other tasks on the film. What Oliver did was much greater. He gave an inspiring, moving performance. All we did was help him finish it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_123_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_123-31">[31]</a></sup> The film is dedicated to Reed's memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p142_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p142-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_authenticity">Historical authenticity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Historical authenticity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="429" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg/180px-Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg/270px-Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg/360px-Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Juba_denarius_in_support_of_Pompey_against_Cesar_60_46_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Juba_I_of_Numidia" title="Juba I of Numidia">Juba</a>. The Numidians were most likely of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> origin, instead of Sub-saharan origin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The film is loosely based on real events that occurred within the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the latter half of the 2nd Century AD. As Ridley Scott wanted to portray Roman culture more accurately than in any previous film, he hired several historians as advisors. Nevertheless, some deviations from historical fact were made to increase interest, maintain narrative continuity, and for practical or safety reasons. Scott also stated that due to the influence of previous films affecting the public perception of what ancient Rome was like, some historical facts were "too unbelievable" to include. For instance in an early version of the script, gladiators would have been carrying out <a href="/wiki/Testimonial" title="Testimonial">product endorsements</a> in the arena; while this would have been historically accurate, it was not filmed for fear that audiences would think it anachronistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>At least one historical advisor resigned due to these changes. Another asked not to be mentioned in the credits (though it was stated in the director's commentary that he constantly asked, "where is the proof that certain things were exactly like they say?"). Historian Allen Ward of the University of Connecticut believed that historical accuracy would not have made <i>Gladiator</i> less interesting or exciting because "creative artists need to be granted some poetic license, but that should not be a permit for the wholesale disregard of facts in historical fiction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GladFilmH_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-GladFilmH-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictionalization">Fictionalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fictionalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> died at <a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a> (a Roman camp on the site of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>) in 180 AD; he was not murdered by his son Commodus following the final battle of the <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a>. In reality Marcus Aurelius gave succession to his immoral son. In doing so the great philosopher emperor ended the beneficent tradition of previous Adoptive Emperors.</p>
<p>The character of Maximus is fictional, although in some respects he resembles the historical figures <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(wrestler)" title="Narcissus (wrestler)">Narcissus</a> (Commodus's real-life murderer and the character's name in the first draft of the screenplay),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exovedate_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-exovedate-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spartacus" title="Spartacus">Spartacus</a> (who led a significant slave revolt), <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Quinctius_Cincinnatus" title="Lucius Quinctius Cincinnatus">Cincinnatus</a> (a farmer who became dictator, saved Rome from invasion, then resigned his six-month appointment after 15 days),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref_c_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_c-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GU_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-GU-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Nonius_Macrinus" title="Marcus Nonius Macrinus">Marcus Nonius Macrinus</a> (a trusted general, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Consul" title="Roman Consul">Consul</a> in 154 AD, and friend of Marcus Aurelius).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter2008_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter2008-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref_2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2008-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PreHistGladiator2012_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-PreHistGladiator2012-42">[42]</a></sup> Although Commodus engaged in show combat in the Colosseum, he was not killed in the arena; he was strangled in his bath by the wrestler Narcissus. Commodus reigned for over twelve years, unlike the shorter period portrayed in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RS_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-RS-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brit_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brit-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The character of Maximus had a similar career (and personality traits as documented by <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Claudius_Pompeianus" title="Tiberius Claudius Pompeianus">Claudius Pompeianus</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syrian</a>) who married <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>' daughter <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Lucilla</a> following the death of <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>. It is believed Aurelius may have wanted Pompeianus to succeed him as <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a> in preference to Commodus but was turned down. Pompeianus had no part in any of the many plots against Commodus. He was not depicted in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the film the character Antonius Proximo claims "the wise" Marcus Aurelius banned gladiatorial games in Rome forcing him to move to <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>. The real Marcus did ban the games but only in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> as punishment for the city's support of the usurper <a href="/wiki/Avidius_Cassius" title="Avidius Cassius">Avidius Cassius</a>. No games were ever banned in Rome. However, when the Emperor started conscripting gladiators into the legions, the resulting shortage in fighters allowed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanista" title="Lanista">lanistae</a></i> such as Proximo to make "windfall" profits through increased charges for their services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In real life, the death of Commodus did not result in a peace for Rome, nor a return to the Roman Republic. Rather, it ushered in a chaotic and bloody power struggle that culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Five_Emperors" title="Year of the Five Emperors">Year of the Five Emperors</a> of AD 193.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anachronisms">Anachronisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Anachronisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Costumes within the film are almost never completely historically correct. Some of the soldiers wear <a href="/wiki/Galea_(helmet)" title="Galea (helmet)">fantasy helmets</a>. The bands wrapped around their lower arms were rarely ever worn. Their appearance is the product of historical movies always depicting peoples of antiquity wearing such bands. Although the film is set within the 2nd century AD, the <a href="/wiki/Lorica_segmentata" title="Lorica segmentata">Imperial Gallic armor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_helmet#Imperial_Gallic" title="Imperial helmet">helmets</a> worn by the legionaries are from AD 75, a century earlier. This was superseded by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_helmet#Imperial_Italic" title="Imperial helmet">new designs</a> in AD 100. Likewise the Legions' <a href="/wiki/Aquilifer" title="Aquilifer">standard bearers (Aquilifer)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centurion" title="Centurion">centurions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_cavalry" title="Roman cavalry">mounted forces</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Auxilia" title="Auxilia">auxiliaries</a> would have worn scale armour: <a href="/wiki/Lorica_squamata" title="Lorica squamata">Lorica squamata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Germanic tribes are dressed in clothes from the stone-age period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_cavalry" title="Roman cavalry">Roman cavalry</a> are shown attacking using <a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">stirrups</a>. This is anachronistic in that the horse-mounted forces of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> used a two-horned saddle. Stirrups were only employed for safety reasons because of the additional training and skill required to ride with a Roman saddle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Catapult" title="Catapult">Catapults</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ballista" title="Ballista">ballistae</a> would not have been used in a forest. They were reserved primarily for <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">sieges</a> and were rarely used in open battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Praetorian Guards seen in the film are all wearing black uniforms. No historical evidence supports that. On Campaign they usually wore standard legionary equipment albeit with some unique decorative elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the bird's eye view of Rome when the city is introduced for the first time there are several buildings that did not exist at the time of <i>Gladiator</i>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Maxentius" title="Basilica of Maxentius">Basilica of Maxentius and Constantine</a> is quite prominent; however, it was not completed until AD 312.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The film's plot was influenced by two 1960s <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a> films of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peplum_film_genre" title="Peplum film genre">sword-and-sandal</a> genre, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire_(film)" title="The Fall of the Roman Empire (film)">The Fall of the Roman Empire</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Spartacus_(film)" title="Spartacus (film)">Spartacus</a>,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and shares several plot points with <i>The Fall of the Roman Empire</i>, which tells the story of Livius, who, like Maximus in <i>Gladiator,</i> is Marcus Aurelius's intended successor. Livius is in love with Lucilla and seeks to marry her while Maximus, who is happily married, was formerly in love with her. Both films portray the death of Marcus Aurelius as an assassination. In <i>Fall of the Roman Empire</i> a group of conspirators independent of Commodus, hoping to profit from Commodus's accession, arrange for Marcus Aurelius to be poisoned; in <i>Gladiator</i> Commodus himself murders his father by smothering him. In the course of <i>Fall of the Roman Empire</i> Commodus unsuccessfully seeks to win Livius over to his vision of empire in contrast to that of his father, but continues to employ him notwithstanding; in <i>Gladiator</i>, when Commodus fails to secure Maximus's allegiance, he executes Maximus's wife and son and tries unsuccessfully to execute him. Livius in <i>Fall of the Roman Empire</i> and Maximus in <i>Gladiator</i> kill Commodus in single combat, Livius to save Lucilla and Maximus to avenge the murder of his wife and son, and both do it for the greater good of Rome.</p>
<p>Scott cited <i>Spartacus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ben-Hur_(1959_film)" title="Ben-Hur (1959 film)">Ben-Hur</a></i> as influences on the film: "These movies were part of my cinema-going youth. But at the dawn of the new millennium, I thought this might be the ideal time to revisit what may have been the most important period of the last two thousand years – if not all recorded history – the apex and beginning of the decline of the greatest military and political power the world has ever known."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_28_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau_28-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Spartacus</i> provides the film's gladiatorial motif, as well as the character of Senator Gracchus, a fictitious senator (bearing the name of a pair of revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">Tribunes</a> from the 2nd century BC) who in both films is an elder statesman of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a> attempting to preserve the ancient rights of the Roman Senate in the face of an ambitious <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocrat</a> –<a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a> in <i>Spartacus</i> and Commodus in <i>Gladiator.</i> Both actors who played Gracchus (in <i>Spartacus</i> and <i>Gladiator</i>), played <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> in previous films – <a href="/wiki/Charles_Laughton" title="Charles Laughton">Charles Laughton</a> of <i>Spartacus</i> played Claudius in the unfinished 1937 film <i><a href="/wiki/I,_Claudius_(film)" title="I, Claudius (film)">I, Claudius</a></i> and Sir <a href="/wiki/Derek_Jacobi" title="Derek Jacobi">Derek Jacobi</a> of <i>Gladiator</i>, played Claudius in <a href="/wiki/I,_Claudius_(TV_series)" title="I, Claudius (TV series)">the 1976 BBC adaptation</a>. Both films also share a specific set piece, wherein a gladiator (Maximus here, <a href="/wiki/Woody_Strode" title="Woody Strode">Woody Strode</a>'s Draba in <i>Spartacus</i>) throws his weapon into a spectator box at the end of a match, as well as at least one line of dialogue: "Rome is the mob", said here by Gracchus and by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> (<a href="/wiki/John_Gavin" title="John Gavin">John Gavin</a>) in <i>Spartacus</i>.</p>
<p>The film's depiction of Commodus's entry into Rome borrows imagery from <a href="/wiki/Leni_Riefenstahl" title="Leni Riefenstahl">Leni Riefenstahl</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazi</a> propaganda film <i><a href="/wiki/Triumph_of_the_Will" title="Triumph of the Will">Triumph of the Will</a></i> (1935), although Scott has pointed out that the <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> of Nazi rallies was itself inspired by the Roman Empire. <i>Gladiator</i> reflects back on the film by duplicating similar events that occurred in <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>'s procession. The Nazi film opens with an aerial view of Hitler arriving in a plane, while Scott shows an aerial view of Rome, quickly followed by a shot of the large crowd of people watching Commodus pass them in a procession with his <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_p114_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_p114-53">[53]</a></sup> The first thing to appear in <i>Triumph of the Will</i> is a Nazi eagle, which is alluded to when a statue of an eagle sits atop one of the arches (and then is shortly followed by several more decorative eagles throughout the rest of the scene) leading up to the procession of Commodus. At one point in the Nazi film, a little girl gives flowers to Hitler, while Commodus is met by several girls who all give him bundles of flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_p115_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_p115-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>The Oscar-nominated score was composed by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Zimmer" title="Hans Zimmer">Hans Zimmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Gerrard" title="Lisa Gerrard">Lisa Gerrard</a>, and conducted by <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Greenaway" title="Gavin Greenaway">Gavin Greenaway</a>. Zimmer was originally planning to use Israeli vocalist <a href="/wiki/Ofra_Haza" title="Ofra Haza">Ofra Haza</a> for the score, after his work with her in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince_of_Egypt" title="The Prince of Egypt">The Prince of Egypt</a></i>. However, Ofra died in her early 40s in late February 2000, before she was able to record, and so Gerrard was chosen instead. Lisa Gerrard's vocals are similar to her own work on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Insider_(film)" title="The Insider (film)">The Insider</a></i> score.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reel_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-reel-55">[55]</a></sup> The music for many of the battle scenes has been noted as similar to <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Gustav Holst</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">Mars: The Bringer of War</a>", and in June 2006, the Holst Foundation sued Hans Zimmer for allegedly copying the late Holst's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priscilla2006_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Priscilla2006-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael2006_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michael2006-57">[57]</a></sup> Another close musical resemblance occurs in the scene of Commodus's triumphal entry into Rome, accompanied by music clearly evocative of two sections – the Prelude to <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Rheingold" title="Das Rheingold">Das Rheingold</a></i> and Siegfried's Funeral March from <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6tterd%C3%A4mmerung" title="Götterdämmerung">Götterdämmerung</a></i> – from <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Der_Ring_des_Nibelungen" title="Der Ring des Nibelungen">Ring of the Nibelung</a></i>. On February 27, 2001, nearly a year after the first soundtrack's release, <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca</a> produced <i><a href="/wiki/Gladiator_(soundtrack)#More_Music_from_Gladiator" title="Gladiator (soundtrack)">Gladiator: More Music From the Motion Picture</a></i>. Then, on September 5, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca</a> produced <i>Gladiator: Special Anniversary Edition</i>, a two-CD pack containing both the above-mentioned releases. Some of the music from the film was featured in the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League_playoffs" title="National Football League playoffs">NFL playoffs</a> in January 2003 before commercial breaks and before and after <a href="/wiki/Half-time" title="Half-time">half-time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winkler_p141_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winkler_p141-58">[58]</a></sup> In 2003, <a href="/wiki/Luciano_Pavarotti" title="Luciano Pavarotti">Luciano Pavarotti</a> released a recording of himself singing a song from the film and said he regretted turning down an offer to perform on the soundtrack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anastasia2003_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anastasia2003-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Gladiator</i> received generally positive reviews, with 76% of the critics polled by <a href="/wiki/Rotten_Tomatoes" title="Rotten Tomatoes">Rotten Tomatoes</a> giving it favorable reviews, with an averaged score of 7.2 out of 10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RT_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-RT-60">[60]</a></sup> At the website <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, which employs a <a href="/wiki/Standard_score" title="Standard score">normalized</a> rating system, the film earned a favorable rating of 64/100 based on 37 reviews by mainstream critics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metacritic_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metacritic-61">[61]</a></sup> The Battle of Germania was cited by <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> as one of their "favorite on-screen battle scenes",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref_2007_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2007-62">[62]</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">Entertainment Weekly</a></i> named Maximus as their sixth favorite action hero, because of "Crowe's steely, soulful performance",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marc2007_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marc2007-63">[63]</a></sup> and named it as their third favorite revenge film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gary2007_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gary2007-64">[64]</a></sup> In December 2000, <i>Gladiator</i> was named the best film of the year by viewers of <i><a href="/wiki/Film..._(TV_programme)" title="Film... (TV programme)">Film 2000</a></i>, taking 40% of the votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In 2002, a <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> (UK TV) poll named it as the sixth greatest film of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-channel4_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-channel4-66">[66]</a></sup> <i>Entertainment Weekly</i> put it on its end-of-the-decade, "best-of" list, saying, "Are you not entertained?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref_f_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_f-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was not without its deriders. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> gave the film 2 out of 4 stars, and criticized the look of the film as "muddy, fuzzy, and indistinct." He also derided the writing, claiming it "employs depression as a substitute for personality, and believes that if characters are bitter and morose enough, we won't notice how dull they are."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebert2000_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebert2000-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Camille_Paglia" title="Camille Paglia">Camille Paglia</a> called the film "boring, badly shot and suffused with sentimental <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">p.c.</a> rubbish."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film earned US$34.83 million on its opening weekend at 2,938 U.S. theaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p141_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p141-70">[70]</a></sup> Within two weeks, the film's box office gross surpassed its US $103 million budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoseWin_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-LoseWin-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FilmsRS_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FilmsRS-72">[72]</a></sup> The film continued on to become one of the highest earning films of 2000 and made a worldwide box office gross of US$457,640,427, with over US$187 million in American theaters and more than the equivalent of US$269 million in non-US markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mojo_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-mojo-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Accolades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_accolades_received_by_Gladiator" title="List of accolades received by Gladiator">List of accolades received by Gladiator</a></div>
<p><i>Gladiator</i> was nominated in 36 individual ceremonies, including the <a href="/wiki/73rd_Academy_Awards" title="73rd Academy Awards">73rd Academy Awards</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Academy_of_Film_and_Television_Arts" title="British Academy of Film and Television Arts">BAFTA Awards</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Awards" title="Golden Globe Awards">Golden Globe Awards</a>. Of 119 award nominations, the film won 48 prizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awards_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-awards-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film won five Academy Awards and was nominated for an additional seven, including <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Screenplay#2000s" title="Academy Award for Best Original Screenplay">Best Original Screenplay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Score#2000s" title="Academy Award for Best Original Score">Best Supporting Actor</a> for <a href="/wiki/Joaquin_Phoenix" title="Joaquin Phoenix">Joaquin Phoenix</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Directing#2000s" title="Academy Award for Best Directing">Best Director</a> for Ridley Scott. It was the first movie to win Best Picture without winning either a directing or screenwriting award since <i><a href="/wiki/All_the_King%27s_Men_(1949_film)" title="All the King's Men (1949 film)">All the King's Men</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/22nd_Academy_Awards" title="22nd Academy Awards">22nd Academy Awards</a> in 1950. In <a href="/wiki/75th_Academy_Awards" title="75th Academy Awards">2003</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_(2002_film)" title="Chicago (2002 film)">Chicago</a></i> became another Best Picture winner which didn't win an Academy Award in either of these two major categories. Due to <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy</a> rules, only <a href="/wiki/Hans_Zimmer" title="Hans Zimmer">Hans Zimmer</a> was officially nominated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Original_Music_Score#2000s" title="Academy Award for Original Music Score">Best Music, Original Score</a>, and not to <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Gerrard" title="Lisa Gerrard">Lisa Gerrard</a> at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> However, the pair did win the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Original Score">Golden Globe Award for Best Original Score</a> as co-composers.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The film's mainstream success is responsible for an increased interest in Roman and classical history in the United States. According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, this has been dubbed the "Gladiator Effect".</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It's called <i>the 'Gladiator' effect</i> by writers and publishers. The snob in us likes to believe that it is always books that spin off movies. Yet in this case, it's the movies – most recently <i>Gladiator</i> two years ago – that have created the interest in the ancients. And not for more Roman screen colossals, but for writing that is serious or fun or both."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytg_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytg-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> biography <i>Cicero: The Life and Times of Rome's Greatest Politician</i> and Gregory Hays's translation of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i> received large spikes in sales after the release of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytg_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytg-83">[83]</a></sup> The film also began a revival of the historical epic genre with films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Troy_(film)" title="Troy (film)">Troy</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Alamo_(2004_film)" title="The Alamo (2004 film)">The Alamo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/King_Arthur_(film)" title="King Arthur (film)">King Arthur</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_(2004_film)" title="Alexander (2004 film)">Alexander</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/300_(film)" title="300 (film)">300</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Heaven_(film)" title="Kingdom of Heaven (film)">Kingdom of Heaven</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood_(2010_film)" title="Robin Hood (2010 film)">Robin Hood</a></i> (the last two were also directed by Scott).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref_2004_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref_2004-84">[84]</a></sup> The character of Maximus was placed 12th in the <i><a href="/wiki/Total_Film" title="Total Film">Total Film</a></i> list of 50 best movie heroes and villains<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and 35th in the <a href="/wiki/Empire_(film_magazine)" title="Empire (film magazine)"><i>Empire</i></a>'s 100 Greatest Movie Characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Maximus is also featured on 55<a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">c</a> "Australian Legends" postage stamp series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-87">[87]</a></sup> Russell Crowe attended a ceremony to mark the creation of the stamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_media">Home media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Home media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The film was first released on <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVD</a> on November 21, 2000, and has since been released in several different extended and special edition versions. Special features for the <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray Disc</a> and DVDs include <a href="/wiki/Deleted_scene" title="Deleted scene">deleted scenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trailer_(promotion)" title="Trailer (promotion)">trailers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Audio_commentary" title="Audio commentary">commentaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Storyboard" title="Storyboard">storyboards</a>, image galleries, <a href="/wiki/Easter_egg_(media)" title="Easter egg (media)">Easter eggs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Audition" title="Audition">cast auditions</a>. The film was released on Blu-ray in September 2009, in a 2-disc edition containing both the theatrical and extended cuts of the film, as part of Paramount's "Sapphire Series" (Paramount bought the DreamWorks library in 2006).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blu-ray_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-blu-ray-88">[88]</a></sup> Initial reviews of the Blu-ray Disc release criticized poor image quality, leading many to call for it to be remastered, as Sony did with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Element" title="The Fifth Element">The Fifth Element</a></i> in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bigscreen_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-bigscreen-89">[89]</a></sup> A remastered version was later released in 2010.</p>
<p>The DVD editions that have been released since the original two-disc version, include a film only single-disc edition as well as a three-disc "extended edition" DVD which was released in August 2005. The extended edition DVD features approximately fifteen minutes of additional scenes, most of which appear in the previous release as deleted scenes. The original cut, which Scott still calls his director's cut, is also select-able via <a href="/wiki/Seamless_branching" title="Seamless branching">seamless branching</a> (which is not included on the UK edition). The DVD is also notable for having a new commentary track featuring director Scott and star Crowe. The film is on the first disc, the second one has a three-hour documentary into the making of the film by DVD producer <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Lauzirika" title="Charles de Lauzirika">Charles de Lauzirika</a>, and the third disc contains supplements. Discs one and two of the three-disc extended edition were also repackaged and sold as a two-disc "special edition" in the EU in 2005.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancelled_sequel">Cancelled sequel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cancelled sequel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In June 2001, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Wick" title="Douglas Wick">Douglas Wick</a> said a <i>Gladiator</i> <a href="/wiki/Prequel" title="Prequel">prequel</a> was in development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stax2001_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stax2001-90">[90]</a></sup> The following year, Wick, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Parkes" title="Walter Parkes">Walter Parkes</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Franzoni" title="David Franzoni">David Franzoni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Logan_(writer)" title="John Logan (writer)">John Logan</a> switched direction to a sequel set fifteen years later;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brian2002_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brian2002-91">[91]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guards</a> rule Rome and an older Lucius is trying to learn who his real father was. However, <a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe" title="Russell Crowe">Russell Crowe</a> was interested in resurrecting Maximus, and further researched Roman beliefs about the afterlife to accomplish this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stax2002_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stax2002-92">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a> expressed interest, although he admitted the project would have to be retitled as it had little to do with gladiators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stax2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stax2003-93">[93]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Easter_egg_(media)" title="Easter egg (media)">easter egg</a> contained on disc 2 of the extended edition / special edition DVD releases includes a discussion of possible scenarios for a follow-up. This includes a suggestion by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Parkes" title="Walter Parkes">Walter F. Parkes</a> that, in order to enable Russell Crowe to return to play Maximus, who dies at the end of the original movie, a sequel could involve a "multi-generational drama about Maximus and the Aureleans and this chapter of Rome", similar in concept to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Godfather_Part_II" title="The Godfather Part II">The Godfather Part II</a></i>.</p>
<p>In 2006, Scott stated he and Crowe approached <a href="/wiki/Nick_Cave" title="Nick Cave">Nick Cave</a> to rewrite the film, but their ideas conflicted with DreamWorks's idea of a spin-off involving Lucius, whom Scott revealed would turn out to be Maximus's son with Lucilla. Scott noted that a tale of corruption in Rome was too complex, whereas <i>Gladiator</i> worked due to its simple drive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 2009, details of Cave's ultimately-rejected script surfaced on the internet: the script having Maximus being reincarnated by the Roman gods and returned to Rome to defend Christians against persecution; then transported to other important periods in history, including <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, and finally being a general in the modern-day <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">Pentagon</a>. This script for a sequel, however, was rejected as being too far-fetched, and not in keeping with the spirit and theme of the original film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaels2009_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michaels2009-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cave_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cave-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gladiator_(2000_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
